        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.9b08378"><meta property="og:title" content="SnO2 Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/acsami.9b08378.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="SnO2 has been extensively studied as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries, which, however, has long been subjected to poor conductivity and large volume expansion accompanied with an unsatisfactory electrochemical performance. Here, novel interlaced SnO2 nanoflakes are synthesized directly on a carbon cloth collector via hydrothermal and annealing treatment and then coated with polypyrrole (PPy) via electrodeposition. The as-prepared flexible SnO2@PPy on the carbon cloth exhibits a high initial capacity of 1172.1 mAh g–1 and an outstanding cycling stability with 85% capacity retention after 300 cycles at 0.1 A g–1, which can be contributed to the interlaced SnO2 nanoflakes as well as the coating of PPy. This result shows promising potential for construction of an electrode in high-performance energy storage fields."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/acsami.9b08378.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ec43a810775c-LHR"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ec43a810775c-LHR">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '609d99be-aefa-4921-abe7-ad67809a5dec'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6ec43a810775c-LHR"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ec43a810775c-LHR">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

Interfaces</i></span><span class="cit-fg-year"> 2019</span><span class="cit-fg-volume">, 11</span><span class="cit-fg-issue">, 27</span><span class="cit-fg-pageRange">, 24198-24204</span></span></span></div></div></div></div></div><input type="hidden" name="aamick" id="copyRights" value="/pb-assets/json/journal-info-1738870756703.json"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone5" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone5"><!-- Article Refresh Event Tracking -->
<script nonce="90f6ec43a810775c-LHR">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoflake Arrays Coated with Polypyrrole on a Carbon Cloth as Flexible Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b08378", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

href="mailto:tujp@zju.edu.cn">tujp@zju.edu.cn</a> (J.T.).</span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Jiangping++Tu" data-id="article_author_search">Jiangping Tu</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" issue&nbsp;</span><time datetime="2019-07-10" class="article__history__time">10 July 2019</time></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D24198%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMinya%2BWang%252C%2BXiuli%2BWang%252C%2BZhujun%2BYao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D27%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b08378%26title%3DSnO2%2BNanoflake%2BArrays%2BCoated%2Bwith%2BPolypyrrole%2Bon%2Ba%2BCarbon%2BCloth%2Bas%2BFlexible%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D24204%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2019" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">SnO<sub>2</sub> has been extensively studied as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries, which, however, has long been subjected to poor conductivity and large volume expansion accompanied with an unsatisfactory electrochemical performance. Here, novel interlaced SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes are synthesized directly on a carbon cloth collector via hydrothermal and annealing treatment and then coated with polypyrrole (PPy) via electrodeposition. The as-prepared flexible SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth exhibits a high initial capacity of 1172.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and an outstanding cycling stability with 85% capacity retention after 300 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which can be contributed to the interlaced SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes as well as the coating of PPy. This result shows promising potential for construction of an electrode in high-performance energy storage fields.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D24198%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMinya%2BWang%252C%2BXiuli%2BWang%252C%2BZhujun%2BYao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D27%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b08378%26title%3DSnO2%2BNanoflake%2BArrays%2BCoated%2Bwith%2BPolypyrrole%2Bon%2Ba%2BCarbon%2BCloth%2Bas%2BFlexible%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D24204%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lamellar+Sno2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lamellar%20SnO2">lamellar SnO<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Conducting+Polymer&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_conducting%20polymer">conducting polymer</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Freestanding&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_freestanding">freestanding</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">To date, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been extensively employed in electric as well as hybrid electric vehicles. However, the resource of lithium is limited, prohibiting the application of LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Thereby, sustainable energy storage devices are crucially needed. Under such a circumstance, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as the most encouraging candidates for LIBs owing to the low cost and abundant reserves of sodium in the environment. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 2 to 4">(2−4)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Over the past decades, tin-based anodes have attracted great interest with high theoretical capacity provided by involved alloying reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> Among them, SnO<sub>2</sub>, with an excellent theoretical capacity of 667 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, has gained special interest as an anode for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> However, SnO<sub>2</sub> faces two challenges, that is, unsatisfactory rate and cyclic performance causing by poor conductivity as well as large volume expansion during cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> Many efforts have been made to solve the problems. One strategy is designing particular shapes including hollow nanoparticles, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> nanosheets, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> nanotubes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> etc., which can enhance the electron/ion transport and relieve the volume expansion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> Although the above-mentioned nanosizing can inhibit pulverization, it is not enough since the intrinsic poor conductivity limits the electron transfer rate in the SnO<sub>2</sub> electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> Another strategy is proposed to combine SnO<sub>2</sub> with conductive materials such as metals, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> and conducting polymers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> which can not only strengthen the conductivity but also reduce the volume change. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> In addition, introducing a dopant such as a metal and nitrogen is also a common strategy to adjust the lattice constant, thus improving the sodium storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Earlier works on the combination of SnO<sub>2</sub> and conductive materials as anodes for SIBs mostly concentrate on powders, which are then mixed with conductive agents and insulating binders and finally coated on current collectors. The insulating additives involved inevitably diminish the energy density of batteries, prohibiting the electron/ion transport as they distribute in SnO<sub>2</sub>. In recent years, fabricating freestanding and flexible electrodes by growing SnO<sub>2</sub> on conductive matrices, mostly carbon substrates, including reduced graphene oxide (rGO) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 31 to 33">(31−33)</a> and biomass-derived carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> is widely taken into account to construct batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p">To date, SnO<sub>2</sub> coated with a conductive layer on a carbon cloth as a freestanding anode for SIBs is still in a blank field. In general, researchers tend to coat active materials with carbon. In this case, however, SnO<sub>2</sub> is prone to partially react with carbon during annealing. Thus, many works focus on coating SnO<sub>2</sub> with conducting polymers rather than carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 36 to 38">(36−38)</a> Usually, conducting polymers are deposited on the SnO<sub>2</sub> surface via hydrothermal treatments, which cannot achieve the uniform distribution of conducting polymers. In such a context, the electrodeposition method emerges to acquire uniform coating with a constant current density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we present a facile method to synthesize SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes deposited on the carbon cloth, which are then coated with PPy, a widely used conducting polymer, via electrodeposition. The electrodeposition here ensures the uniform coating of PPy on the SnO<sub>2</sub> surface. The freestanding composite is designed to present lamellar shapes, which prevent active materials from aggregation when synergized with the PPy wrap. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 40 to 42">(40−42)</a> Furthermore, PPy is able to avoid the pulverization of SnO<sub>2</sub>, the direct contact between SnO<sub>2</sub> and electrolyte, and strengthen the conductivity in the meanwhile. With these merits mentioned above, the flexible SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode displays high cycle retention and rate capability. Our SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy anode based on the carbon cloth for SIBs provides useful instructions in the design of foremost electrodes for energy storage.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">The carbon cloth was first cleaned by continuously sonicating in ethanol for 15 min and then decorated with hydrophilic group under a plasma instrument for 15 min. Through a hydrothermal method, SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes were then grown on the pretreated carbon cloth followed by annealing. In a typical preparation of SnO<sub>2</sub>, 1 g of urea, 1 mL of hydrochloric acid (37 wt %), and 20 mL of mercaptoacetic acid were added to 80 mL of deionized water. Then, 0.2 g of tin dichloride dehydrate was added to the resulting solution, which was moved to a Teflon-lined autoclave after intense stirring. The pretreated carbon cloth (4 × 2 cm<sup>2</sup> in size) was immersed in the solution. The autoclave was then heated in an electrical oven at 120 °C for 12 h and cooled immediately under flowing water. The carbon cloth with SnO<sub>2</sub> was rinsed with deionized water to remove impurities. After completely drying at 60 °C, it was finally annealed at 500 °C for 3 h in argon to improve its conductivity.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes on the carbon cloth then served as the skeleton for the growth of PPy. The electrolyte for electropolymerization of PPy was prepared by adding 1 mL of pyrrole and 0.2 g of <i>p</i>-toluenesulfonic acid (TsOH) into 100 mL of phosphate buffer solution containing 96 mM sodium dihydrogen phosphate (NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>) and 104 mM disodium hydrogen phosphate dodecahydrate (Na<sub>2</sub>HPO<sub>4</sub>·12H<sub>2</sub>O). The electropolymerization of PPy was conducted via a chronopotentiometry method at a three-compartment system with the SnO<sub>2</sub> on the carbon cloth as the working electrode, Pt foil as the counterelectrode, and Hg/HgCl<sub>2</sub> as the reference electrode at a current density of 1.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 3000 s.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A scanning electron microscope (SEM, Hitachi S4800) was employed to characterize the morphologies of the composite with an energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscope (EDS). The structure was further analyzed via a transmission electron microscope (TEM, Tecnai G2 F20 S-TWIN). X-ray diffraction (XRD) was applied to determine the crystal phases of the composite using a X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku D/Max-2550PC, Cu Kα radiation, λ = 0.154184 nm, 2θ = 10–80°). Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy was carried out on a Nicolet iS50 FTIR spectrometer to characterize the composition of composite with KBr pellets. In addition, a Raman spectroscope (HR-800, argon laser) was used with 514 nm excitation laser (from 800 to 2000 cm<sup>–1</sup>). Through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), further information for the composition was acquired with an ESCALAB 250Xi photoelectron spectrometer, the excitation source of which is Al Kα X-ray. The thermogravimetric (TGA) analysis was recorded from room temperature to 900 °C in O<sub>2</sub> with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> on a thermal analyzer (Netzsch STA 449C).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth acted as additive-free anodes for SIBs. NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1.0 M) in a 1:1 (v/v) mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) with 5 vol % of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was employed as the electrolyte. With sodium metal sheets as the counterelectrodes, the coin-type cells (CR 2025) were assembled in the glove box, utilizing glass microfiber (Whatman GF/F) filters as the separator. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were recorded at a potential range of 0.005–2.5 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) utilizing a CHI 660D electrochemical workstation. The cycle and rate performance were examined on a LAND battery test system. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were conducted from 100 kHz to 10 mHz. For comparison, half coin cells using the SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes on the carbon cloth as anodes were also assembled with the same electrolyte and counterelectrodes by the same procedures.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the preparation process of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth. Through a hydrothermal procedure, interlaced SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes were uniformly grown on the carbon cloth. After annealing in Ar, the morphology of the products remained unchanged. Then, the PPy shell was uniformly wrapped on the surface of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes, providing a good confinement of SnO<sub>2</sub>. The uniform dispersion of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes on the carbon cloth is demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. Moreover, the enlarged SEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c shows that the thickness of SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes is approximately 10 nm. In addition, PPy is also uniformly deposited on SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes with a thickness of ∼20 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d,e), which makes the surface of SnO<sub>2</sub> rougher. Moreover, from the EDS mapping images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f, the homogeneous distribution of C, N, Sn, and O in the sample confirms the successful fabrication of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Preparation schematics of the lamellar SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite on the carbon cloth. SEM images of (b, c) SnO<sub>2</sub> and (d, e) SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite on the carbon cloth, and (f) EDS images of C, N, Sn, and O elements in the SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The microstructures of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy and SnO<sub>2</sub> are further investigated by TEM analysis. Interlaced SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, which are well consistent with the SEM observation. The enlarged images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c display that lamellar SnO<sub>2</sub> is wrapped by an amorphous layer, which is a PPy shell, while SnO<sub>2</sub> shows no shell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d–f). As shown in the high-resolution TEM images, the inner layer shows a clear lattice distance of 0.33 nm, belonging to the (110) crystal plane of the SnO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> In addition, the outer layer, PPy with a thickness of 8–10 nm, is homogeneously deposited.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM and HRTEM images of (a–c) SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite and (d–f) SnO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The crystal phase of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth are determined by XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). A sharp peak at about 25° and a weak peak at around 43° are the specific peaks of graphite (JCPDS 75-1621), which should be ascribed to the carbon cloth. Additional eight peaks are well indexed to the tetragonal SnO<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS 41-1445) for the XRD pattern of the SnO<sub>2</sub> electrode. After the decoration of PPy, the XRD pattern remains the same, verifying the unchanged crystal structure of SnO<sub>2</sub> and the amorphous characteristic of PPy. To further verify the successful decoration of PPy, FTIR characterizations of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth are carried out (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). For both SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy, two peaks at 564 and 630 cm<sup>–1</sup> are associated with the Sn–O bond. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Additionally, specific peaks of PPy are observed in SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy. These four weak peaks are, namely, 797 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to the C–H out-of-plane bending vibration at the β-position of the pyrrole ring, 1263 cm<sup>–1</sup> attributing to the N–H deformation vibration, 1465 cm<sup>–1</sup> due to the C–N stretching vibration, and 1576 cm<sup>–1</sup> belonging to the C═C stretching vibration of the pyrrole ring. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> Since the peaks of PPy are all weak, the FTIR spectra ranging from 700 to 1700 cm<sup>–1</sup> are provided in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b to distinguish the differences between SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy more clearly. The Raman spectra of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy all present two peaks at about 1340 and 1590 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), belonging to the D-band and graphitic G-band, respectively. From SnO<sub>2</sub> to SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio increases, further proving the existence of amorphous PPy because of the defects brought by PPy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Besides, the peak at ∼630 cm<sup>–1</sup> belongs to the stretching vibration (A<sub>1g</sub>) of SnO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Phase and composition characterizations of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth: (a) XRD patterns, (b) FTIR spectra, and (c) Raman spectra. XPS spectra of the SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite: (d) Sn 3d, (e) O 1s, and (f) N 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The compositions and surface functional groups of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy are studied by XPS analysis. C, Sn, O, and N are clearly identified in the survey spectrum of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The C 1s spectrum in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> can be separated into two peaks, including a sharp peak of C–C (284.6 eV) and a broad peak of C–N (285.6 eV) introduced by nitrogen in PPy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d represents the Sn 3d spectrum with two peaks at 486.73 and 495.14 eV, which are associated with Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, the O 1s spectrum contains different O bonds including Sn–O (530.57 eV), Sn–O–C (531.57 eV), and C═O (532.45 eV). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Moreover, the N 1s spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f contains two peaks of pyrrolic N (399.85 eV) and C–N<sup>+</sup> (401.79 eV), confirming the existence of PPy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the TGA measurements of CC@SnO<sub>2</sub> and CC@SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy are conducted (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). In contrast, the temperature of CC@SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy at which the sample starts to lose weight is clearly lesser than that of CC@SnO<sub>2</sub> (TGA curve inset). Consider that the oxidation temperature of PPy starts earlier than that of CC, the contents of SnO<sub>2</sub> and PPy can be calculated to be 6.41 and 2 wt %, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy and SnO<sub>2</sub> are investigated as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The carbon cloth offers nearly no CV area and active redox couple with a potential range of 0.005–2.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), suggesting that the carbon cloth here does not provide sodium storage, which can be also verified by its cycle performance at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). In contrast, the CV profiles of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrodes show obvious redox peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b) and their CV curves are similar. In the initial cathodic scan, three peaks located at 1, 0.75, and 0.005 V can be discovered. The broad peak at 1 V is related to the transformation of SnO<sub>2</sub> into Sn, which disappears in the following cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="reaction 1">reaction </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>), indicating that this reaction is irreversible. The peaks at 0.75 and 0.005 V correspond to the appearance of amorphous Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn (0 ≤ <i>x</i> &lt; 3.75) and crystalline Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="reaction 2">reaction </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>), respectively. In the initial anodic scan, peaks at 0.43 and 1.15 V are associated with the reversible dealloying of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn (0 ≤ <i>x</i> &lt; 3.75), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 35, 53 and 54">(35,53,54)</a> The involved charge/discharge reactions are verified by ex situ XRD characterizations at different voltages during the initial cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). For the fresh electrode, there are mainly three peaks at 26.6, 33.9, and 37.9°, corresponding to SnO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS 41-1445). During the sodiation process, the peaks of Sn emerge and then disappear, accompanied by the increase of peak densities of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn. After the desodiation process, the peak densities of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn gradually decrease with the reappearance of specific peaks of Sn.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>From the second cycle, CV profiles of subsequent cycles are nearly overlapped, suggesting a good cyclic stability of SnO<sub>2</sub>. Note that the peak current densities of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode are much higher than those of SnO<sub>2</sub>, indicating that the deposition of PPy is beneficial to accelerate the electrochemical process of the SnO<sub>2</sub> electrode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. CV curves of (a) SnO<sub>2</sub> and (b) SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Charge/discharge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> of (c) SnO<sub>2</sub> and (d) SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy at different cycles. (e) Comparison of cycle performance of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> with the Coulombic efficiency. (f) Comparison of rate capability of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy at different current densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Ex situ XRD characterizations of the SnO<sub>2</sub> electrode at different voltages in the initial cycle. Phases of Sn, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn, and SnO<sub>2</sub> are assigned to PDF card nos. 86-2264, 31-1327, and 41-1445, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The first charge/discharge profiles of both SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy and SnO<sub>2</sub> on the carbon cloth at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> to further prove the significance of PPy. Since PPy provides negligible capacity compared to that of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrodes according to the charge/discharge curves of CC@PPy, the electrochemical performance of both two samples discussed below can be determined according to the mass of SnO<sub>2</sub>. The SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode shows a high initial discharge capacity of 1172.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a charge capacity of 414.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, much higher than those of the SnO<sub>2</sub>, which delivers a first discharge capacity of 676.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a charge capacity of 154.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The loss of capacity here is deduced to the irreversible reaction mentioned above (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="reaction 1">reaction </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>) and the appearance of solid electrolyte interphase layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref55" aria-label="reference 8 and 55">(8,55)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d displays the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, 50th, and 100th charge/discharge curves of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy on the carbon cloth, respectively. The charge and discharge plateaus are consistent with the corresponding CV curves. The discharge plateau at 0.75 V in the initial cycle disappears in the following cycles, which is the same as the disappearance of the cathodic peak at 0.75 V. The charge/discharge profiles of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy are almost overlapped, indicating an excellent reversibility compared to that of SnO<sub>2</sub>. After 100 cycles, the SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode exhibits a high discharge capacity of 413.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (113 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for SnO<sub>2</sub>). Even after 300 successive cycles, the SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode still delivers 403.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with 85% capacity retention (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), superior to SnO<sub>2</sub> with 43% capacity retention as well as previous works (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). Moreover, the Coulombic efficiency of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite increases in the following cycles, superior to that of the control sample, suggesting better cyclic reversibility compared to that of SnO<sub>2</sub>. The enhanced Coulombic efficiency can be attributed to PPy, which can avoid a direct contact between SnO<sub>2</sub> and the electrolyte. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f demonstrates the rate capacities of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite on the carbon cloth. Obviously, the SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite shows a considerably higher capacity than that of SnO<sub>2</sub> at various current densities. As the current density increases from 0.1 to 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode delivers around 450, 315, 150, and 75 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The discharge capacity can be restored to 530 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> as the current density recovers from 1 to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, showing a good structure stability of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy. Note that the capacity enhances when the current density approaches back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which can be associated with the excellent confinement of SnO<sub>2</sub> resulting from PPy and also the good conductivity of PPy to facilitate the reactions at high current densities with good ion/electron transport. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref56" aria-label="reference 6 and 56">(6,56)</a></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">PCNF@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">374</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">82.7</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnO<sub>2</sub>@rGO</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">330</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">150</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">81.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">honeycomb-inspired&nbsp;SnO<sub>2</sub>@C&nbsp;nanospheres</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">251.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&lt;68</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">MWNTs@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.05</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">60</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">76</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">N-doped&nbsp;CNF@SnO<sub>2</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">270</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">403.7</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">85</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">this work</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The improved ion/electron transport can be further evidenced by EIS measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). Both the electrodes present typical Nyquist plots with a semicircle and then a straight line, which link to charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg impedance, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy (225.6 Ω) is much lower than that of SnO<sub>2</sub> (482.7 Ω), revealing better conductivity brought by PPy decoration. Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) can be determined according to the following equations <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 58 and 59">(58,59)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the room temperature, <i>A</i> is the contact area of the electrode, <i>n</i> is the number of electron transportation, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, and <i>C</i> is the molar concentration of the sodium ion. The Warburg impedance coefficient, σ<sub>w</sub>, here is acquired by fitting the straight line in the low-frequency regions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). The slope of the fitting line is σ<sub>w</sub> according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>. Thus, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy can be calculated to be 1 × 10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, much higher than that of SnO<sub>2</sub> (2.43 × 10<sup>–16</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). This result can be ascribed to the strengthened electronic conductivity brought by the PPy shell, which accelerates the chemical reaction kinetics.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/medium/am-2019-08378m_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Nyquist plots of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrodes before cycling. (b) Related linear fitting line.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/asset/images/large/am-2019-08378m_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b08378&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Based on the above discussion, the enhanced electrochemical performance derived from the introduction of the PPy shell can be summarized as follows. First, PPy offers a conductive shell for SnO<sub>2</sub> to strengthen the electronic conductivity of the whole electrode. Second, the PPy coating acts as a buffer for the volume expansion of active materials and can effectively prohibit them from pulverization as well as aggregation in the meanwhile, leading to outstanding cyclic stability. In addition, PPy can restrain the side reactions by preventing SnO<sub>2</sub> from a direct contact with the electrolyte, which results in improved Coulombic efficiency.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a rational design of lamellar SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite on a carbon cloth is proposed, which is proven to show improved electrochemical performance for SIBs. The designed interlaced SnO<sub>2</sub> nanosheet arrays not only shorten the path of ion transport but also raise the contact area of active materials and electrolyte. Combined with good conductivity and confinement of SnO<sub>2</sub> brought by the PPy shell, the flexible SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrodes demonstrate good rate capability, high capacity (1172.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at first cycle), and an excellent cycle stability (85% capacity retention after 300 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Our work paves a way for constructing high-performance electrodes in SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b08378" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b08378</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">FTIR spectra of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composites; survey spectrum of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composites; XPS spectrum of SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composites: C 1s; TGA curves of CC@SnO<sub>2</sub> and CC@SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composites and enlarged curves (inset); CV curves at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> of carbon cloth for SIBs; cycling performance of the carbon cloth at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; and comparison of the first charge/discharge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrodes for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b08378/suppl_file/am9b08378_si_001.pdf">am9b08378_si_001.pdf (271.36 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b08378" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Optimization of SnO2 Nanoparticles Confined in a Carbon Matrix towards Applications as High-Capacity Anodes in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shenying; Chu, Shiyong; Lu, Qian; Zhou, Wei; Cai, Rui; Shao, Zongping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemistrySelect</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2/carbon composites including amorphous carbon and graphene or carbon nanotubes are synthesized by a gas-liq. interfacial approach and subsequent annealing process.  The effect of the carbon source and the conductive additive on the electrochem. performance is investigated by galvanostatic charge-discharge tests.  SnO2@Glucose/Graphene (SnO2@Glu/G) composites as anodes of sodium-ion batteries show the best electrochem. performance, delivering 306 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1 between 0.01-3 V, while exhibiting 278 and 226 mA h g-1 at 1 and 2 A g-1, resp.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
