                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Honeycomb-inspired design of ultrafine SnO2@C nanospheres embedded in carbon film as anode materials for high performance lithium- and sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ao, Xiang; Jiang, Jianjun; Ruan, Yunjun; Li, Zhishan; Zhang, Yi; Sun, Jianwu; Wang, Chundong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">359</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">340-348</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin oxide (SnO2) has been considered as one of the most promising anodes for advanced rechargeable batteries due to its advantages such as high energy d., earth abundance and environmental friendly.  However, its large vol. change during the Li-Sn/Na-Sn alloying and de-alloying processes will result in a fast capacity degrdn. over a long term cycling.  To solve this issue, in this work we design and synthesize a novel honeycomb-like composite composing of carbon encapsulated SnO2 nanospheres embedded in carbon film by using dual templates of SiO2 and NaCl.  Using these composites as anodes both in lithium ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries, no discernable capacity degrdn. is obsd. over hundreds of long term cycles at both low c.d. (100 mA g-1) and high c.d. (500 mA g-1).  Such a good cyclic stability and high delivered capacity have been attributed to the high cond. of the supported carbon film and hollow encapsulated carbon shells, which not only provide enough space to accommodate the vol. expansion but also prevent further aggregation of SnO2 nanoparticles upon cycling.  By engineering electrodes of accommodating high vol. expansion, we demonstrate a prototype to achieve high performance batteries, esp. high-power batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph-wEpi1Icy7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljETPjGZBjXKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXovFGitL4%253D&amp;md5=7df9aec3316f2da344bf045a01e6c44b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.12.118"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bian, H.                         cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced conversion reaction kinetics in low crystallinity SnO2/CNT anodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cui, Jiang; Xu, Zheng-Long; Yao, Shanshan; Huang, Jiaqiang; Huang, Jian-Qiu; Abouali, Sara; Garakani, Mohammad Akbari; Ning, Xiaohui; Kim, Jang-Kyo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The specific capacities of SnO2 anodes in sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are far below the values expected from theory.  Herein, we propose that the kinetically-controlled, reversible 'conversion reaction' between Na ions and SnO2 is responsible for Na ion storage in SnO2 anodes where the ion diffusion rate is the limiting factor.  This revelation is contrary to the current understanding of the 'alloying reaction' as the major reaction process.  Aiming to fully utilize the theor. capacity from the conversion reaction, a composite electrode consisting of carbon nanotubes coated with a mainly amorphous SnO2 phase together with cryst. nanoparticles is synthesized.  The SnO2/CNT anodes deliver a superior specific capacity of 630.4 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 and 324.1 mA h g-1 at a high rate of 1.6 A g-1 due to the enhanced kinetics.  The vol. expansion of the composite is accommodated by the CNT substrate, giving rise to an excellent 69% capacity retention after 300 cycles.  The aforementioned findings give new insight into the fundamental understanding of the electrochem. kinetics of SnO2 electrodes and offer a potential soln. to the low capacity and poor cyclic stability of other metal oxide anodes based on conversion reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVlevjzntL_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljJYKnCbW0o9A"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of porous carbon sphere@SnO2@carbon layer coating composite as high performance anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xin; Sun, Xiaohong; Gao, Zhiwen; Hu, Xudong; Guo, Jingdong; Cai, Shu; Guo, Ruisong; Ji, Huiming; Zheng, Chunming; Hu, Wenbin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>

                        cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SnO2 particles anchored on N-doped graphene surface as sodium-ion battery anode with enhanced electrochemical capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Gui-Zhi; Feng, Jian-Min; Dong, Lei; Li, Xi-Fei; Li, De-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The small-size SnO2 particles uniformly dispersed on the nitrogen doping graphene nanocomposites (SnO2-N-GNS) have been successfully synthesized by a mild hydrothermal method with the precursors of graphene oxide, urea and SnCl.45H2O.  The phase (XRD, EDX and XPS) and morphol. (SEM, TEM) anal. further confirm the obtained products.  When the composite served as anode material in sodium ion batteries (SIBs), it delivers superior sodium storage performance with reversible discharge capacity of 294.4 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 in the voltage range from 0.01-3 V after 50 discharge/charge cycles, which is much higher than that of SnO2-GNS (182.9 mAh g-1) and N-GNS (114.9 mAh g-1). Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">18387</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">18396</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-Confined SnO2-Electrodeposited Porous Carbon Nanofiber Composite as High-Capacity Sodium-Ion Battery Anode Material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dirican, Mahmut; Lu, Yao; Ge, Yeqian; Yildiz, Ozkan; Zhang, Xiangwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18387-18396</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium resources are inexpensive and abundant, and hence, sodium-ion batteries are promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries.  However, lower energy d. and poor cycling stability of current sodium-ion batteries prevent their practical implementation for future smart power grid and stationary storage applications.  Tin oxides (SnO2) can be potentially used as a high-capacity anode material for future sodium-ion batteries, and they have the advantages of high sodium storage capacity, high abundance, and low toxicity.  However, SnO2-based anodes still cannot be used in practical sodium-ion batteries because they experience large vol. changes during repetitive charge and discharge cycles.  Such large vol. changes lead to severe pulverization of the active material and loss of elec. contact between the SnO2 and carbon conductor, which in turn result in rapid capacity loss during cycling.  Here, we introduce a new amorphous carbon-coated SnO2-electrodeposited porous carbon nanofiber (PCNF@SnO2@C) composite that not only has high sodium storage capability, but also maintains its structural integrity while ongoing repetitive cycles.  Electrochem. results revealed that this SnO2-contg. nanofiber composite anode had excellent electrochem. performance including high-capacity (374 mAh.g-1), good capacity retention (82.7%), and large Coulombic efficiency (98.9% after 100th cycle).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrH0ehkIAKCjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDSYVXd8e0eQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht12ksL3K&amp;md5=8de376bdd85bc64574f66b549117f13e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit63" data-doi="10.1007/s40820-017-0172-2"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Growth of SnO<sub>2</sub> Nanoflowers on N-doped Carbon Nanofibers as Anode for Li- and Na-ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano-Micro Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">21</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s40820-017-0172-2</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D21%26journal%3DNano-Micro%2BLett.%26author%3DJ.%2BLiang%26author%3DC.%2BYuan%26author%3DH.%2BLi%26author%3DK.%2BFan%26author%3DZ.%2BWei%26author%3DH.%2BSun%26author%3DJ.%2BMa%26title%3DGrowth%2Bof%2BSnO2%2BNanoflowers%2Bon%2BN-doped%2BCarbon%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BLi-%2Band%2BNa-ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs40820-017-0172-2&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b08378&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs40820-017-0172-2&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit63R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">63</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Growth of SnO2 nanoflowers on N-doped carbon nanofibers as anode for Li- and Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Jiaojiao; Yuan, Chaochun; Li, Huanhuan; Fan, Kai; Wei, Zengxi; Sun, Hanqi; Ma, Jianmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano-Micro Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">21/1-21/9</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NLAEBV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2150-5551</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nano-Micro Letters</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is urgent to solve the problems of the dramatic vol. expansion and pulverization of SnO2 anodes during cycling process in battery systems.  To address this issue, we design a hybrid structure of N-doped carbon fibers@SnO2 nanoflowers (NC@SnO2) to overcome it in this work.  The hybrid NC@SnO2 is synthesized through the hydrothermal growth of SnO2 nanoflowers on the surface of N-doped carbon fibers obtained by electrospinning.  The NC is introduced not only to provide a support framework in guiding the growth of the SnO2 nanoflowers and prevent the flower-like structures from agglomeration, but also serve as a conductive network to accelerate electronic transmission along one-dimensional structure effectively.  When the hybrid NC@SnO2 was served as anode, it exhibits a high discharge capacity of 750 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 100 cycles in Li-ion battery and 270 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 for 100 cycles in Na-ion battery, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqrTQfTUj6hDrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDSYVXd8e0eQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitFGrs7fL&amp;md5=eaf00bc74a8e00de90fdd1b0099d68ab</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2/carbon composites including amorphous carbon and graphene or carbon nanotubes are synthesized by a gas-liq. interfacial approach and subsequent annealing process.  The effect of the carbon source and the conductive additive on the electrochem. performance is investigated by galvanostatic charge-discharge tests.  SnO2@Glucose/Graphene (SnO2@Glu/G) composites as anodes of sodium-ion batteries show the best electrochem. performance, delivering 306 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1 between 0.01-3 V, while exhibiting 278 and 226 mA h g-1 at 1 and 2 A g-1, resp.  The mechanism of improved electrochem. performance for graphene is researched in detail.  The results reveal a porous structure with fine SnO2 particles due to the introduction of graphene oxide, and an effective electron charge transfer network from the graphene increases its reversible capacity, rate performance and cycling performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqykEVnhZ8tCLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgXxYWYeqa7-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXnslSktrg%253D&amp;md5=c0bb76251639f5c96936f59a5296ac7b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgXxYWYeqa7-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2017.12.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhan, J.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring hydrogen molybdenum bronze for sodium ion storage: Performance enhancement by vertical graphene core and conductive polymer shell</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhan, Jiye; Deng, Shengjue; Zhong, Yu; Wang, Yadong; Wang, Xiuli; Yu, Yan; Xia, Xinhui; Tu, Jiangping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rational construction of high-performance electrode materials is crucial for advancement of sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  In this work, for the first time, we explore the sodium ion storage performance of hydrogen molybdenum bronze (HMB).  To perfect its performance, we controllably sandwich HMB into conductive vertical graphene (VG) skeleton and poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) shell forming free-standing VG/HMB/PEDOT composite arrays with the help of powerful successive electrodeposition methods.  HMB is completely compatible with VG and PEDOT, and intimately combined with them.  Due to the unique integrated porous structure and omnibearing conductive network, the designed VG/HMB/PEDOT composite arrays show high sodium ion storage capacities (385 mA h g-1 at 200 mA g-1) and superior long-term cycling stability (320 mA h g-1 at 200 mA g-1 after 500 cycles) when used as anode of SIBs, much better than the VG/HMB and HMB/PEDOT counterparts.  Our proposed fabrication strategy offers a new route for designing high-performance electrodes for applications in electrochem. energy storage and electrocatalysis.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3gTLcW8p1kLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgXxYWYeqa7-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvFyisr7L&amp;md5=e7b1490a2f9bb7dedcb792ee41427ff9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.11.074"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>309</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">238</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">244</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.11.074</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Scalable preparation of porous micron-SnO2/C composites as high performance anode material for lithium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Ming-Shan; Lei, Ming; Wang, Zhi-Qiang; Zhao, Xing; Xu, Jun; Yang, Wei; Huang, Yun; Li, Xing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">309</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">238-244</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nano tin dioxide-carbon (SnO2/C) composites prepd. by various carbon materials, such as carbon nanotubes, porous carbon, and graphene, have attracted extensive attention in wide fields.  However, undesirable concerns of nanoparticles, including in higher surface area, low tap d., and self-agglomeration, greatly restricted their large-scale practical applications.  In this study, novel porous micron-SnO2/C (p-SnO2/C) composites are scalable prepd. by a simple hydrothermal approach using glucose as a carbon source and Pluronic F127 as a pore forming agent/soft template.  The SnO2 nanoparticles were homogeneously dispersed in micron carbon spheres by assembly with F127/glucose.  The continuous three-dimensional porous carbon networks have effectively provided strain relaxation for SnO2 vol. expansion/shrinkage during lithium insertion/extn.  In addn., the carbon matrix could largely minimize the direct exposure of SnO2 to the electrolyte, thus ensure formation of stable solid electrolyte interface films.  Moreover, the porous structure could also create efficient channels for the fast transport of lithium ions.  As a consequence, the p-SnO2/C composites exhibit stable cycle performance, such as a high capacity retention of over 96% for 100 cycles at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1 and a long cycle life up to 800 times at a higher c.d. of 1000 mA g-1.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmFNaEYAc_YbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhGpf5gXmJ8SA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xisl2ht7k%253D&amp;md5=92137d91530b423763d2e03cb0556628</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201500097"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.                         rightTab-cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Confined annealing-induced transformation of tin oxide into sulfide for sodium storage applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Xuming; Xiao, Fengping; Wang, Shuo; Liu, Jin; Leung, Michael K. H.; Yu, Denis Y. W.; Rogach, Andrey L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As conversion-type anode materials for Na ion batteries, metal sulfides have a lower theor. capacity but generally deliver a much higher reversible capacity in practice than their oxide counterparts.  The origin of this phenomenon was not fundamentally understood yet.  From a reaction enthalpy anal., the authors reveal that the sulfidation reaction with S is possible for a wide variety of metal oxides.  The authors proceeded to convert the less anode-active Sn oxide/C (SnO2/C) composites into the highly anode-active Sn sulfide/C (SnS2/C) materials by confined annealing with S powder.  For anode applications in Na ion batteries, SnS2/C delivers a reversible capacity of 770 mA h g-1, which is more than double the value of SnO2/C (360 mA h g-1).  The obsd. superiority of SnS2/C anodes is correlated with the narrower band gap, higher conversion potential that allows for a larger polarization, and more energetically favorable conversion process on the surface of SnS2.  In particular, the authors demonstrate a modeling strategy for surface conversion reactions, which could potentially be considered for the kinetics study of various conversion-type anode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo6tVVHSnBOArVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhGpf5gXmJ8SA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXotFWqu78%253D&amp;md5=aa76c6447317d89e9d9edee11e15f64a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C3TA13592F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn oxide (SnO2) is regarded as a promising anode material for both Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and Na-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its large theor. capacity.  However, poor elec. cond. and the weak cyclability resulted from dramatic vol. expansion upon cycling process still hinder its practical application.  Herein, the authors report a facile two-step hydrothermal route to encapsulate core-shell structured C nanotube (CNT)@SnO2 composite in a graphene coating with novel three-dimensional (3D) porous framework architecture (CNT@SnO2@G) as anode for both LIBs and SIBs. Na storage testing suggests that a high discharge capacity of 323 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 25 mA g-1 was achieved. Through TEM and HRTEM observations on time-dependent samples, the as-prepd. SnO2 NSs were assembled by oriented attachment of preformed SnO2 nanoparticles (NPs).  Systematic trials showed that well-defined ultrathin SnO2 NSs could only be obtained under appropriate reaction time, solvent, additive, precursor concn., and cooling rate.  A certain degree of nonstoichiometry appears inevitable in the well-defined SnO2 NSs sample.  However, deviations from the optimal synthetic parameters give rise to severe nonstoichiometry in the products, giving Sn3O4 or SnO.  This finding may open new accesses to the fundamental studies of Sn oxides as well as their intertransition processes.  Finally, the authors studied the Li-ion storage of the SnO2 NSs as compared to SnO2 hollow spheres and NPs.  The results showed superior performance of SnO2 NSs sample over its two counterparts.  This greatly enhanced Li-ion storage capability of SnO2 NSs is probably resulting from the ultrathin thicknesses and the unique porous structures: the nanometer-sized networks provide negligible diffusion times of ions thus faster phase transitions, while the breathable interior porous structure can effectively buffer the drastic vol. changes during lithiation and delithiation reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_qllqur3xdLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lji4mYCIqPNJw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XosFCiuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=a7c4eacc446a30499588ac7c89a8c886</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1021/ja909321d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin oxide (SnO) is considered one of the most promising metal oxides for utilization as anode material in sodium ion batteries (SIBs), because of its ease of synthesis, high specific gravimetric capacity, and satisfactory cycling performance.  However, to aim at practical applications, the Coulombic efficiency during cycling needs to be further improved, which requires a deeper knowledge of its working mechanism.  Here, a microflower-shaped SnO material is synthesized by means of an ultrafast ionic liq.-assisted microwave method.  The as-prepd. SnO anode active material exhibits excellent cycling performance, good Coulombic efficiency as well as a large capacity delivered at low potential, which is fundamental to maximize the energy output of SIBs.  These overall merits were never reported before for pure SnO anodes (i.e., not in a composite with, for example, graphene).  Addnl., by combining ex situ XRD and XPS, it is clearly demonstrated for the first time that the Sn-Na alloy, which is formed during the initial sodium sodiation, desodiates in two successive but fully sepd. steps.  Totally different from the previous report, the pristine SnO phase is not regenerated upon desodiation up to 3 V vs Na/Na+.  The newly disclosed reaction route provides an alternative view of the complex reaction mechanism of these families of metal oxides for sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpoyJ3FH4dtL7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWjYkpNhy-bg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXht1Sjt7bO&amp;md5=1ce9f539539ae7ac5d490485ee0ae1be</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201300105"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, H.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Exploring advanced high-rate anodes is of great importance for the development of next-generation high-power lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  Here, novel carbon nanotubes (CNTs)/Li4Ti5O12 (LTO) core/shell arrays on carbon cloth (CC) as integrated high-quality anode are constructed via a facile combined chemical vapor deposition-atomic layer deposition (ALD) method.  ALD-synthesized LTO is strongly anchored on the CNTs' skeleton forming core/shell structures with diameters of 70-80 nm the combined advantages including highly conductive network, large surface area, and strong adhesion are obtained in the CC-LTO@CNTs core/shell arrays.  The electrochemical performance of the CC-CNTs/LTO electrode is completely studied as the anode of LIBs and it shows noticeable high-rate capability (a capacity of 169 mA h g(-1) at 1 C and 112 mA h g(-1) at 20 C), as well as a stable cycle life with a capacity retention of 86% after 5000 cycles at 10 C, which is much better than the CC-LTO counterpart.  Meanwhile, excellent cycling stability is also demonstrated for the full cell with LiFePO4 cathode and CC-CNTs/LTO anode (87% capacity retention after 1500 cycles at 10 C).  These positive features suggest their promising application in high-power energy storage areas.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcQUHzZbA_-aq3tlzo9xastyfW6udTcc2eYtBieP9txVpLntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1MnmsFyhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=981d955361a54d968cec67b6f7b94724</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA05069G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X. SnO2 nanoparticles (5 nm in diam.) are homogeneously attached to the surface of graphene sheets without self-aggregation.  The heterostructured SnO2/rGO xerogel possesses numerous micron-sized pores that can efficiently buffer the volumetric change of SnO2 during the charge/discharge process and provide multidimensional channels, improving the cond. between active materials and electrolyte.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Heteroatom-doping was regarded as an effective strategy to regulate the Na storage performance of C materials.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2/graphene materials have received extensive attention in broad applications owning to their excellent performances.  However, multi-step and harsh synthetic methods with high temps. and high pressures are major obstacles that need to be overcome.  Herein a simple, low-cost, and scalable approach is proposed to construct ultrafine SnO2/graphene nanomaterials effectively under const. pressure and at the low temp. of 80 °C for 4 h, in which ultrafine SnO2 nanoparticles grow on graphene sheets uniformly and firmly via Sn-O-C bonding.  This result depends on the synergetic effect of two reactions, the redn. of graphene oxide and formation of SnO2 nanoparticles, which are achieved successfully.  More importantly, the constructed SnO2/graphene material exhibits excellent electrochem. properties in both lithium-ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries.  As an anode material for lithium-ion batteries, it displays a high reversible capacity (1420 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 after 90 cycles) and good cycling life (97% at 1 A g-1 after 230 cycles), whereas in sodium-ion batteries, it maintains a capacity of 1280 mA h g-1 at 0.05 A g-1 and 650 mA h g-1 at 0.2 A g-1 after 90 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 is a promising material for both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries owing to its high theor. capacities.  Unfortunately, the electrochem. performance of SnO2 is unsatisfactory because of the large vol. change that occurs during cycling, low electronic cond. of inactive oxide matrix, and poor kinetics, which are particularly severe in Na-ion batteries.  Herein, ultra-fine SnO2 nanocrystals anchored on a unique three-dimensional (3D) porous reduced graphene oxide (rGO) matrix are described as promising bifunctional electrodes for Li-ion and Na-ion batteries with excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  Ultra-fine SnO2 nanocrystals of size ∼6 nm are well-coordinated to the graphene sheets that comprise the 3D macro-porous structure.  Notably, superior rate capability was obtained up to 3 C (1/n C is a measure of the rate that allows the cell to be charged/discharged in n h) for both batteries.  In situ X-ray diffractometry measurements during lithiation (or sodiation) and delithiation (or desodiation) were combined with various electrochem. techniques to reveal the real-time phase evolution.  This crit. information was linked with the internal resistance, ion diffusivity (DLi+ and DNa+), and the unique structure of the composite electrode materials to explain their excellent electrochem. performance.  The improved capacity and superior rate capabilities demonstrated in this work can be ascribed to the enhanced transport kinetics of both electrons and ions within the electrode structure because of the well-interconnected, 3D macro-porous rGO matrix.  The porous rGO matrix appears to play a more important role in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), where the larger mass/radius of Na-ions are marked concerns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqidfVgimZOI7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lho47Zl-rveKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhsVGksbjI&amp;md5=83af205a83c8bc1959d522415686bb4a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201702225"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The large vol. variations of tin-based oxides hinder their extensive application in the field of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 hollow spheres (HSs) were synthesized by a hydrothermal route by use of an org. additive (2-mercaptopropionic acid or MPA) and a cationic surfactant (cetyltrimethyl ammonium bromide or CTAB).  The progressive transformation of SnO2 solid spheres to SnO2 HSs 140-150 nm in dimensions wherein a thin shell of densely packed SnO2 crystallites with a tetragonal crystal structure surrounds an empty core was followed by scanning- and transmission-electron microscopy.  The roles of MPA as the HS structure-directing agent, CTAB as the moiety which prevents HS aggregation, and water as the solvent crucial for hollow core formation were independently detd. by elaborate morphol. analyses.  With the goal of realizing superior electrochem. performance, hybrids of optimized SnO2 HSs embedded in graphene oxide (GO) nanosheets and enveloped by a sheath of a conducting polymer, poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) or PEDOT, were also synthesized; the continuity of the amorphous PEDOT coating on SnO2 HS/GO was confirmed by elemental mapping and XPS.  Galvanostatic charge-discharge studies revealed an initial reversible capacity of 990 mA h g-1 for SnO2 HSs at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, and a capacity of 400 mA h g-1 was retained after 30 cycles.  A significant improvement in cycling performance was achieved in the SnO2 HS/GO/PEDOT hybrid, as the synergy between the moderately high intrinsic electronic cond. of GO nanosheets and the ability of PEDOT to buffer the vol. change during repetitive Li+ charge-discharge more efficiently compared to pristine SnO2 HS impart a capacity of 608 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 to the hybrid, retained at the end of 150 cycles, and the latter value was ∼1248 mA h g-1 when the mass of only the SnO2 HS in the hybrid was considered.  The SnO2 HS/GO/PEDOT hybrid also showed an excellent rate capability as a capacity of 381 mA h g-1 was attained even at a high c.d. of 2000 mA g-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 with high theor. capacity has long suffered from its instinct large vol. variation and low elec. transport linked to poor cycling stability and rate performance.  Here we present the heterogeneous branched core-shell SnO2-PANI nanorod arrays which have been successfully designed and fabricated by an efficient and facile hydrothermal treatment followed by electrodeposition.  The heterogeneous core-shell SnO2-PANI nanorod arrays exhibit a high reversible capacity of 506 mAh/g after 100 cycles, resulting the capacity fading of 0.579% per cycle between 20 and 100 cycles, much lower than that of nanosheet-assembled hierarchal SnO2-PANI nanorod arrays (1.150%) and bare SnO2 nanorod arrays (1.151%).  At high c.d. of 3000 mA/g, heterogeneous core-shell SnO2-PANI nanorod arrays maintain a capacity of 660 mAh/g after the c.d. returns to 100 mA/g, 3 times as high as that of nanosheet-assembled hierarchal SnO2-PANI nanorod arrays (206 mAh/g) and 6 times as high as that of bare SnO2 nanorod (124 mAh/g).  The enhanced electrochem. performance can be attributed to the branched conductive PANI shells, which not only release the stress of vol. expansion and maintain mech. integrity during cycling, but also realize three dimensional transport for electrons.  Our work demonstrates a great potential for the application of heterogeneous branched core-shell PANI-SnO2 nanorod arrays for lithium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqPB49HkBb6ybVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li5e4_SR4SP6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtlOmtL3N&amp;md5=42690cb95f09b24fd072a2321173da47</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201810729"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Polypyrrole (PPy) was synthesized by chem. oxidative polymn. technique using monomer pyrrole and ammonium persulfate as an oxidant in a ratio of 1:1. The FTIR spectra shows that the presence of all characteristics absorption peaks of polypyrrole i.e., 890 cm-1 (=C-H out-of-plane vibration), 1040 cm-1 (=C-H in-plane vibration), 1170 cm-1 (N-C stretch bending) and 1410 cm-1 (pyrrole ring vibration).             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene has exceptional electronic, optical, mech. and thermal properties, which provide it with great potential for use in electronic, optoelectronic and sensing applications.  The chem. functionalization of graphene was studied with a view to controlling its electronic properties and interactions with other materials.  Covalent modification of graphene by org. diazonium salts was used to achieve these goals, but because graphene comprises only a single at. layer, it is strongly influenced by the underlying substrate.  Here, the authors show a Stark difference in the rate of electron-transfer reactions with org. diazonium salts for monolayer graphene supported on a variety of substrates.  Reactions proceed rapidly for graphene supported on SiO2 and Al2O3 (sapphire), but negligibly on alkyl-terminated and hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) surfaces, as shown by Raman spectroscopy.  The authors also develop a model of reactivity based on substrate-induced electron-hole puddles in graphene, and achieve spatial patterning of chem. reactions in graphene by patterning the substrate.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQYXd7YFJl3bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgDd6aweofMfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtFOgtrbM&amp;md5=9cae91dfb1930855e06a54d1df3396ed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1039/c2an36059d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon nanodots (C-Dots) as a new form of carbonaceous nanomaterials have aroused much interest and intensive research due to their inspiring properties.  Compared to traditional semiconductor quantum dots, these newly emergent nanodots possess a no. of advantageous characteristics, among which low-toxicity is particularly fascinating.  More and more research into C-Dots have focused on synthesis methods and biol.-related applications.  Microwave-assisted approaches have attracted attention because microwave treatment can provide intensive and efficient energy, and as a consequence shorten the reaction time.  The authors designed a "green", rapid, eco-friendly and waste-reused approach to synthesize fluorescent and water-sol. C-Dots from eggshell membrane (ESM) ashes according to a microwave-assisted process.  ESM selected as the carbon source was a common protein-rich waste in daily life and can be obtained easily and cheaply.  The C-Dots from the authors' method showed the maximal fluorescence emission peak at 450 nm and the fluorescence quantum yield was ∼14%.  The authors further designed a sensitive probe for glutathione based on the fluorescence turn off and on of the C-Dots-Cu2+ system, which showed a linear range of 0.5-80 μmol L-1 and detection limit of 0.48 μmol L-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We explore a hybrid material consisting of SnO2 nanoparticles (NPs) embedded in the porous shells of carbon cages (SnO2-PSCC).  The hybrid material exhibits improved kinetics of lithiation-delithiation and high reversible capacity, and excellent cyclic stability without capacity loss over 100 cycles at 500 mA g-1 with a coulombic efficiency close to 100% after the initial cycle.  This can be ascribed to the high elec. cond., the hierarchical porosity and the confinement effect of the PSCC on the vol. change of SnO2 NPs.  The material has a large reversible capacity of 460 mA h g-1 at a high c.d. of 5 A g-1 due to a short ion diffusion length in the bulk and large no. of inter-pore ion transport channels.  These results provide insight into improving the lithium storage performance of SnO2 by facilitating the reaction kinetics and indicate that this hybrid material has great potential for use in high-rate and durable lithium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqsZT4u5tswSrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgDd6aweofMfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvFGlurc%253D&amp;md5=3cc7762bfa04437dac732448002d4444</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b08901"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible polypyrrole (PPy) films with highly ordered structures were fabricated by a novel vapor phase polymn. (VPP) process and used as the anode material in Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and Na-ion batteries (SIBs).  The PPy films demonstrate excellent rate performance and cycling stability.  At a charge/discharge rate of 1 C, the reversible capacities of the PPy film anode reach 284.9 and 177.4 mAh/g in LIBs and SIBs, resp.  Even at a charge/discharge rate of 20 C, the reversible capacity of the PPy film anode retains 54.0 and 52.9% of the capacity of 1 C in LIBs and SIBs, resp.  After 1000 electrochem. cycles at a rate of 10 C, there is no obvious capacity fading.  The mol. structure and electrochem. behaviors of Li- and Na-ion doping and dedoping in the PPy films are studied by XPS and ex situ XRD.  It is believed that the PPy film electrodes in the overoxidized state can be reversibly charged and discharged through the doping and dedoping of Li or Na ions.  Because of the self-adaptation of the doped ions, the ordered pyrrolic chain structure can realize a fast charge/discharge process.  This result may substantially contribute to the progress of research into flexible polymer electrodes in various types of batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo-CXU7OX5Yq7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgDd6aweofMfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitVSrsbjI&amp;md5=9d548f514f3b364ed646cf02862b4567</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref51" class="rightTabRefNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit51" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta01515g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vinayan, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report a facile strategy to synthesize SnO2 nanoparticles dispersed N doped graphene (SnO2/NG). The SnO2 nanoparticles are dispersed over N doped graphene by a modified polyol redn. method.  The dispersed SnO2 nanoparticles are 2-3 nm in size with homogeneous dispersion and good crystallinity.                         rightTab-cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Honeycomb-inspired design of ultrafine SnO2@C nanospheres embedded in carbon film as anode materials for high performance lithium- and sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ao, Xiang; Jiang, Jianjun; Ruan, Yunjun; Li, Zhishan; Zhang, Yi; Sun, Jianwu; Wang, Chundong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin oxide (SnO2) has been considered as one of the most promising anodes for advanced rechargeable batteries due to its advantages such as high energy d., earth abundance and environmental friendly.  However, its large vol. change during the Li-Sn/Na-Sn alloying and de-alloying processes will result in a fast capacity degrdn. over a long term cycling.  To solve this issue, in this work we design and synthesize a novel honeycomb-like composite composing of carbon encapsulated SnO2 nanospheres embedded in carbon film by using dual templates of SiO2 and NaCl.  Using these composites as anodes both in lithium ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries, no discernable capacity degrdn. is obsd. over hundreds of long term cycles at both low c.d. (100 mA g-1) and high c.d. (500 mA g-1).  Such a good cyclic stability and high delivered capacity have been attributed to the high cond. of the supported carbon film and hollow encapsulated carbon shells, which not only provide enough space to accommodate the vol. expansion but also prevent further aggregation of SnO2 nanoparticles upon cycling.  By engineering electrodes of accommodating high vol. expansion, we demonstrate a prototype to achieve high performance batteries, esp. high-power batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph-wEpi1Icy7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liXszKhZQx_7g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXovFGitL4%253D&amp;md5=7df9aec3316f2da344bf045a01e6c44b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.12.118"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bian, H.         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The specific capacities of SnO2 anodes in sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are far below the values expected from theory.  Herein, we propose that the kinetically-controlled, reversible 'conversion reaction' between Na ions and SnO2 is responsible for Na ion storage in SnO2 anodes where the ion diffusion rate is the limiting factor.  This revelation is contrary to the current understanding of the 'alloying reaction' as the major reaction process.  Aiming to fully utilize the theor. capacity from the conversion reaction, a composite electrode consisting of carbon nanotubes coated with a mainly amorphous SnO2 phase together with cryst. nanoparticles is synthesized.  The SnO2/CNT anodes deliver a superior specific capacity of 630.4 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 and 324.1 mA h g-1 at a high rate of 1.6 A g-1 due to the enhanced kinetics.  The vol. expansion of the composite is accommodated by the CNT substrate, giving rise to an excellent 69% capacity retention after 300 cycles.  The aforementioned findings give new insight into the fundamental understanding of the electrochem. kinetics of SnO2 electrodes and offer a potential soln. to the low capacity and poor cyclic stability of other metal oxide anodes based on conversion reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVlevjzntL_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCiTjtRzwqiQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVWntrvK&amp;md5=91fc426bcb151fcc899df4e769eb7de4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2017.10.107"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fabrication of Porous Carbon Sphere@SnO<sub>2</sub>@Carbon Layer Coating Composite as High Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Appl. Surf. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>433</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">713</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">722</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.apsusc.2017.10.107</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of porous carbon sphere@SnO2@carbon layer coating composite as high performance anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xin; Sun, Xiaohong; Gao, Zhiwen; Hu, Xudong; Guo, Jingdong; Cai, Shu; Guo, Ruisong; Ji, Huiming; Zheng, Chunming; Hu, Wenbin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">433</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">713-722</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASUSEE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0169-4332</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 has triggered lots of research efforts as anode for sodium-ion batteries.  However, the vol. expansion and poor cond. lead to an unsatisfactory electrochem. performance for the practical application of SnO2.  In this work, a novel carbon-coated SnO2 supported by porous carbon sphere composite is synthesized by hydrothermal process combining with annealing method.  The porous carbon sphere@SnO2@carbon layer coating composite anode delivers a reversible capacity of 326 mAh g-1 over 80 cycles at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1.  Even at 1600 mA g-1, a capacity of 82 mAh g-1 is still maintained after 550 cycles.  Such excellent performance can be ascribed to the unique structure, which efficiently accommodates vol. expansion, enhances cond. and offers shortened sodium-ion transport pathway.  The charge-storage mechanisms can be comprised of diffusion-controlled reaction and pseudocapacitance effect.  At high scan rate of 1.0 mV s-1, the capacity contribution of pseudocapacitance effect could reach as high as 78%.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoH5xSg69hNvrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCiTjtRzwqiQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs12rt7rP&amp;md5=97d1d069c3087716c09c7114c62553d1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2016.10.109"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X. F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">SnO<sub>2</sub> Particles Anchored on N-Doped Graphene Surface as Sodium-Ion Battery Anode with Enhanced Electrochemical Capability</span>. <i>Appl. Surf. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>396</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">269</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">277</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.apsusc.2016.10.109</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SnO2 particles anchored on N-doped graphene surface as sodium-ion battery anode with enhanced electrochemical capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Gui-Zhi; Feng, Jian-Min; Dong, Lei; Li, Xi-Fei; Li, De-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">396</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">269-277</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASUSEE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0169-4332</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The small-size SnO2 particles uniformly dispersed on the nitrogen doping graphene nanocomposites (SnO2-N-GNS) have been successfully synthesized by a mild hydrothermal method with the precursors of graphene oxide, urea and SnCl.45H2O.  The phase (XRD, EDX and XPS) and morphol. (SEM, TEM) anal. further confirm the obtained products.  When the composite served as anode material in sodium ion batteries (SIBs), it delivers superior sodium storage performance with reversible discharge capacity of 294.4 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 in the voltage range from 0.01-3 V after 50 discharge/charge cycles, which is much higher than that of SnO2-GNS (182.9 mAh g-1) and N-GNS (114.9 mAh g-1).  Besides, the SnO2-N-GNS electrode also exhibits admirable rate stability with the discharge capacity of 206 mAh g-1 even at 800 mA g-1.  More importantly, the SnO2-N-GNS shows lower internal resistance and larger sodium-ion diffusion coeff.  These enhanced electrochem. performances of SnO2-N-GNS are due to the N-doping defects and the homogeneous dispersion of SnO2 particles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq1ELCuRjLA_rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCiTjtRzwqiQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslKjs7zO&amp;md5=dbc819b290f4f49cc07f669523bba9f3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref62" class="rightTabRefNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit62" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b04338"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dirican, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yildiz, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon-Confined SnO<sub>2</sub>-Electrodeposited Porous Carbon Nanofiber Composite as High-Capacity Sodium-Ion Battery Anode Material</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">18387</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">18396</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.5b04338</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-Confined SnO2-Electrodeposited Porous Carbon Nanofiber Composite as High-Capacity Sodium-Ion Battery Anode Material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dirican, Mahmut; Lu, Yao; Ge, Yeqian; Yildiz, Ozkan; Zhang, Xiangwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18387-18396</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium resources are inexpensive and abundant, and hence, sodium-ion batteries are promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries.  However, lower energy d. and poor cycling stability of current sodium-ion batteries prevent their practical implementation for future smart power grid and stationary storage applications.  Tin oxides (SnO2) can be potentially used as a high-capacity anode material for future sodium-ion batteries, and they have the advantages of high sodium storage capacity, high abundance, and low toxicity.  However, SnO2-based anodes still cannot be used in practical sodium-ion batteries because they experience large vol. changes during repetitive charge and discharge cycles.  Such large vol. changes lead to severe pulverization of the active material and loss of elec. contact between the SnO2 and carbon conductor, which in turn result in rapid capacity loss during cycling.  Here, we introduce a new amorphous carbon-coated SnO2-electrodeposited porous carbon nanofiber (PCNF@SnO2@C) composite that not only has high sodium storage capability, but also maintains its structural integrity while ongoing repetitive cycles.  Electrochem. results revealed that this SnO2-contg. nanofiber composite anode had excellent electrochem. performance including high-capacity (374 mAh.g-1), good capacity retention (82.7%), and large Coulombic efficiency (98.9% after 100th cycle).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrH0ehkIAKCjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCiTjtRzwqiQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht12ksL3K&amp;md5=8de376bdd85bc64574f66b549117f13e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref63" class="rightTabRefNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit63" data-doi="10.1007/s40820-017-0172-2"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Growth of SnO<sub>2</sub> Nanoflowers on N-doped Carbon Nanofibers as Anode for Li- and Na-ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano-Micro Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">21</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s40820-017-0172-2</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit63R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">63</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Growth of SnO2 nanoflowers on N-doped carbon nanofibers as anode for Li- and Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Jiaojiao; Yuan, Chaochun; Li, Huanhuan; Fan, Kai; Wei, Zengxi; Sun, Hanqi; Ma, Jianmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano-Micro Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">21/1-21/9</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NLAEBV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2150-5551</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nano-Micro Letters</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is urgent to solve the problems of the dramatic vol. expansion and pulverization of SnO2 anodes during cycling process in battery systems.  To address this issue, we design a hybrid structure of N-doped carbon fibers@SnO2 nanoflowers (NC@SnO2) to overcome it in this work.  The hybrid NC@SnO2 is synthesized through the hydrothermal growth of SnO2 nanoflowers on the surface of N-doped carbon fibers obtained by electrospinning.  The NC is introduced not only to provide a support framework in guiding the growth of the SnO2 nanoflowers and prevent the flower-like structures from agglomeration, but also serve as a conductive network to accelerate electronic transmission along one-dimensional structure effectively.  When the hybrid NC@SnO2 was served as anode, it exhibits a high discharge capacity of 750 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 100 cycles in Li-ion battery and 270 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 for 100 cycles in Na-ion battery, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqrTQfTUj6hDrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCiTjtRzwqiQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitFGrs7fL&amp;md5=eaf00bc74a8e00de90fdd1b0099d68ab</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i14">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b08378" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b08378</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">FTIR spectra of SnO<sub>2</sub> and SnO<sub>2</sub>@PPy composites; (function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	});

	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	});

		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

<script nonce="90f6ec43a810775c-LHR">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {

    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>

    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
